Raising Hands in Prayer: The Hanafi Position and the Debate with al-Awza'i
Hanafi ruling on raising hands and the dispute with al-Awza'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raising hands in prayer and explain why the Hanafi school prefers narrators with deeper fiqh over higher chains in this dispute.

Core Idea
Raising hands in prayer is sunnah only at specific occasions; the Hanafi school relies on narrators with greater fiqh knowledge.

The act of raising hands (raf' al-yadayn) during prayer is a point of difference between the Hanafi and other schools. This lesson examines the Hanafi position, the list of occasions where raising hands is confirmed sunnah, and the famous debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam al-Awza'i. You will learn the Hanafi principle of preferring narrators based on their fiqh, and how this shapes the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that raising hands is a confirmed sunnah only at the opening takbir and the other listed occasions, not at ruku or rising from it.
The list includes: opening takbir, qunut in witr, Eid takbirs, seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, on Safa and Marwah, at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after stoning jamrahs, and during tasbih after prayers.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that al-Awza'i (and later Shafi'is) hold that hands are raised at ruku and rising from it as well.
3
Analyze the proofs
Compare the two hadiths: Hanafi proof via Alqamah (Prophet only raised at opening) vs. Shafi'i proof via Ibn Umar (Prophet raised at ruku). Note the chain difference.
The Hanafi hadith is narrated by Hammad and Ibrahim; the Shafi'i by al-Zuhri and Salim.
4
Understand the Hanafi tarjih
Learn the principle of tarjih bi fiqh al-ruwat: preference is given to narrators with deeper understanding of fiqh, not necessarily the highest chain.
Abu Hanifa argued Hammad was more knowledgeable in fiqh than Salim, and Alqamah and Ibrahim were also superior jurists.
5
Note the practical consequence
Hanafis do not raise hands at ruku; Shafi'is and Malikis do. This affects congregational prayer in mixed-madhhab settings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسنّ رفع اليدين إلا عند افتتاح كل صلاة، المصلي وعند تكبير القنوت في الوتر وتكبيرات الزوائد في العيدين وحين يرى الكعبة، وحين أي وقت معاينتها يستلم الحجر الأسود، وحين يقوم على الصفا والمروة، وعند الوقوف بعرفة ومزدلفة، وبعد رمي الجمرة الأولى والوسطى، وعند التسبيح عقب الصلوات، وإذا فرغ الرجل من سجدتي الركعة الثانية افترش رجله اليسرى وجلس عليها، ونصب يمناه، ووجه ولو كان المصلي مقتدياً أصابعها نحو القبلة، ووضع يديه على فخذيه وبسط أصابعه، والمرأة تتورك . عن عبد الله بن مسعود للنهوض : أي للقيام للركعة الثانية [مراقي الفلاح] بلا اعتماد إلخ رد على الشافعي حيث ذهب إلى أنه يعتمد بيديه على الأرض ويجلس جلسة خفيفة تسمى جلسة استراحة كالأولى: أي فيما قدمناه الأركان من والواجبات والسنن والأداب [البحر الرائق] لا يثني : أي لا يأتي بدعاء الاستفتاح. [البحر الرائق] ولا يسن رفع إلخ :أي أفاد أنه لا يرفع يديه على وجه السنة المؤكدة إلا في هذه المواضع، وليس مراده النفي مطلقاً؛ لأن رفع الأيدي وقت الدعاء مستحب كما عليه المسلمون في سائر البلاد. [البحر الرائق: ٦١٦/١] إلا عند افتتاح إلخ في المسألة حكاية روي أن الأوزاعي لقي أبا حنيفة له في المسجد الحرام، فقال: ما بال أهل العراق لا يرفعون أيديهم عندا الركوع وعند رفع الرأس منه، وقد حدثني الزهري عن سالم عن ابن عمر أنه علي كان يرفع يديه عندهما، فقال أبو حنيفة: حدثني حماد عن إبراهيم عن علقمة أن النبي كان يرفع يديه عند تكبيرة الافتتاح، ثم لا يعود، فقال الأوزاعي: عجبا من أبي حنيفة: أحدثه بحديث الزهري عن سالم، وهو يحدثني بحديث حماد عن إبراهيم، فرجح حديثه بعلو إسناده، فقال أبو حنيفة: أما حماد فكان كان أفقه من سالم، ولو لا سبق ابن عمر ما لقلت بأن علقمة أفقه منه، الزهري، وإبراهيم وأما عبد الله فعبد الله، فرجح حديثه بفقه الرواة، وهو المذهب، فإن الترجيح بفقه الرواة، لا بعلو الإسناد. [العناية: ٢٦٩/١] والكلام في هذا الموضع كثير ، وهذا المختصر لا يحتمله خلا أن المعتمد الرواة، ورواة أخبارنا البدريون من أصحاب رسول صلى الله عليه وسلم الذين كانوا يلون النبي في الصلاة، ورواته : ابن عمر ووائل بن حجر كانوا يقومون ببعد منه الضلع البلا، والأخذ بقول الأقرب أولى، وروي عن ابن عباس لما أنه قال: العشرة الذين شهد لهم النبي بالجنة لم يكونوا يرفعوا أيديهم إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة. (العناية)
And raising the hands is not sunnah except at the opening of every prayer, the worshiper, and at the takbir of qunut in witr and the additional takbirs in the two Eids, and when he sees the Ka'bah, i.e., at the time of seeing it, when he touches the Black Stone, when he stands on Safa and Marwah, at the standing at Arafah and Muzdalifah, after throwing the first and middle jamrah, and at the tasbih after the prayers. And when the man finishes the two prostrations of the second rak'ah, he spreads his left foot and sits on it, and erects his right foot, and directs its toes towards the qibla even if the worshiper is a follower, and places his hands on his thighs and spreads his fingers. And the woman sits mutawarrikan (on her left hip). From Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. 'To stand up': i.e., to stand for the second rak'ah [Maraqi al-Falah] 'Without leaning etc.' is a response to al-Shafi'i who held that he leans on his hands on the ground and sits a brief sitting called the resting sitting, like the first: i.e., in what we have mentioned of the pillars, obligations, sunan, and etiquettes [al-Bahr al-Raiq] 'He does not recite thana': i.e., he does not come with the opening supplication. [al-Bahr al-Raiq] 'And raising is not sunnah etc.': i.e., it informed that he does not raise his hands in a confirmed sunnah manner except in these places, and his intention is not absolute negation; because raising hands during supplication is recommended as the Muslims in all lands do. [al-Bahr al-Raiq: 1/616] 'Except at the opening etc.': regarding this issue, there is a story: It is narrated that al-Awza'i met Abu Hanifa in the Sacred Mosque and said: What is wrong with the people of Iraq that they do not raise their hands at the bowing and at raising the head from it, and al-Zuhri narrated to me from Salim from Ibn Umar that he used to raise his hands at those two times? Abu Hanifa said: Hammad narrated to me from Ibrahim from Alqamah that the Prophet used to raise his hands at the opening takbir and then did not return. Al-Awza'i said: I am amazed at Abu Hanifa: I narrate to him a hadith of al-Zuhri from Salim, and he narrates to me a hadith of Hammad from Ibrahim. So he preferred his hadith based on high chain. Abu Hanifa said: As for Hammad, he was more knowledgeable in fiqh than Salim, and were it not for Ibn Umar's precedence, I would have said that Alqamah is more knowledgeable than him [al-Zuhri?], and Ibrahim [is more knowledgeable], and as for Abdullah, that is Abdullah. So he preferred his hadith based on the fiqh of the narrators, and that is the madhhab, for the preference is by the fiqh of the narrators, not by high chain. [al-Inayah: 1/269] And the discussion in this place is extensive, and this summary cannot contain it, except that the relied-upon are the narrators, and the narrators of our reports are the Badriyyun (those who attended Badr) from the Companions of the Messenger of Allah who used to stand close to the Prophet in prayer, and his narrators: Ibn Umar and Wa'il ibn Hujr used to stand at a distance from him? The taking of the statement of the nearer is more appropriate. And it is narrated from Ibn Abbas that he said: The ten who were promised Paradise by the Prophet never raised their hands except at the opening of prayer. (al-Inayah)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage contains a well-known Hanafi ruling on raising hands (raf' al-yadayn) in prayer, followed by the famous debate between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam al-Awza'i. The passage begins by stating that raising hands is not a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) except at specific places: the opening takbir of every prayer, the qunut takbir in witr, the additional takbirs in Eid prayers, when seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, on Safa and Marwah, at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after stoning the first and middle jamrahs, and during tasbih after prayers. This is the Hanafi position. The commentary notes that this does not negate the general recommendation of raising hands during supplication. Then the passage narrates the historical encounter between Abu Hanifa and al-Awza'i in the Sacred Mosque. Al-Awza'i challenged the Iraqi practice of not raising hands at ruku and rising from it, citing a hadith from Ibn Umar via al-Zuhri and Salim. Abu Hanifa responded with a hadith from Alqamah via Hammad and Ibrahim that the Prophet only raised hands at the opening takbir. When al-Awza'i argued that his chain was higher (a'la), Abu Hanifa countered that preference should be given to the fiqh of the narrators, not the height of the chain. He argued that Hammad was more knowledgeable in fiqh than Salim, and the narrators of the Hanafi hadiths (including Alqamah and Ibrahim) were also more juristically skilled. This principle of preferring a narrator with deeper fiqh is part of Hanafi usul. The passage ends with a reference to the companions who were close to the Prophet in prayer. After this discussion, the passage describes the sitting for tashahhud at the end of the second rak'ah: the man spreads his left foot and sits on it, erects his right foot with toes pointing to the qibla, and places his hands on his thighs spreading fingers; the woman sits mutawarrikan (on her left hip).

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): The act of raising hands to the ears during prayer, sunnah at specific places only.
الترجيح بفقه الرواة (al-tarjih bi fiqh al-ruwat): The principle of preferring a hadith based on the narrators' knowledge of fiqh, even if the chain is less high.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it sunnah to raise the hands in prayer?
A) At the opening takbir and at every ruku
B) At the opening takbir only
C) At the opening takbir and at a few other specific occasions like Eid takbirs
D) Never

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, raising hands in prayer is a confirmed sunnah only at specific occasions; the ruling is based on hadith narrated by Companions with deeper fiqh, and the dispute with other schools is resolved by preferring narrators with greater juristic insight.

The Sitting for Tashahhud in the Hanafi School
Description of sitting posture for tashahhud and response to Shafi'i on leaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the correct sitting posture (iftrash for men, tawarruk for women) in the prayer and explain the Hanafi ruling on leaning on hands when standing up.

Core Idea
Men sit on their left foot, women sit on their left hip; leaning on hands when standing up is not sunnah in the Hanafi school.

After completing the two prostrations of the second rak'ah, the worshiper sits for the tashahhud. This lesson details the sunnah posture for men and women, and addresses the minor dispute about whether to lean on the hands when standing up for the next rak'ah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The sitting (jalsah) for tashahhud after the second prostration is a wajib act; the specific posture is sunnah mu'akkadah.
The passage describes the sunnah posture for men and women.
2
List the sunnah posture for men
Men spread the left foot and sit on it, erect the right foot with toes towards the qibla, place hands on thighs with fingers spread.
This is called iftrash.
3
List the sunnah posture for women
Women sit mutawarrikan (on the left hip) with both feet protruding to the right side.
This is called tawarruk.
4
Examine the dispute about leaning on hands
The passage responds to al-Shafi'i's view that one should lean on hands when standing up. The Hanafi position is to stand without leaning.
The Hanafi proof is from Ibn Mas'ud's practice.
5
Note the practical consequence
Shafi'is perform a brief resting sitting (jalsah istirahah) leaning on hands; Hanafis do not and consider it makruh.

Standing for the Second Rak'ah and the Ruling on Raising Hands
Ruling on standing without leaning and occasions for raising hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method of standing for the second rak'ah, list the occasions where raising hands is sunnah mu'akkadah, and explain the Hanafi proof against raising hands at bowing and rising.

Core Idea
The Prophet ﷺ raised his hands only at the opening takbir; Hanafis restrict raising hands to specific occasions.

In this lesson, we examine the rulings for standing after prostration for the second rak'ah and the occasions when raising the hands is emphasized. The Hanafi school holds that one should stand without leaning or a brief rest, and that raising hands is not sunnah mu'akkadah at bowing and rising. This position is supported by a hadith from 'Alqamah and the practice of the Badri companions, debated with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of standing
Recognize that standing for the second rak'ah is done by takbir, without leaning on the hands and without a brief sitting (jalsat al-istiraha). This is a distinct Hanafi ruling.
The phrase 'bila i'timad' (without leaning) refutes al-Shafi'i's allowance.
2
Note the exception for the second rak'ah
The second rak'ah is like the first except that Thana' (opening supplication) and Ta'awwudh are not repeated.
Thana' is only recited in the first rak'ah according to Hanafis.
3
List the occasions for raising hands
Enumerate the specific occasions where raising hands is sunnah mu'akkadah: opening takbir of every prayer, takbir of qunut in witr, extra takbirs in Eid, seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, standing on Safa and Marwah, at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after throwing the first and middle jamrah, and during tasbih after prayers.
Raising hands at bowing and rising is not among these occasions.
4
Examine the proof and debate
Follow the hadith of 'Alqamah (via Hammad and Ibrahim) that the Prophet raised his hands only at the opening takbir, and the rebuttal to al-Awza'i's hadith of Ibn 'Umar. Abu Hanifah argued that preference is given based on the fiqh of narrators, not merely chain height.
The Badri companions' practice is also cited as evidence.
5
Apply the ruling
State the mufta bihi: hands are not raised at bowing or rising from bowing. In practice, a Hanafi worshipper raises hands only at the specified occasions.
This is the relied-upon position of the school.

ثم رفع رأسه مكبّرا للنهوض بلا اعتماد على الأرض بيديه وبلا قعود، والركعة الثانية كالأولى، إلا أنه لا يثني ولا يتعوّذ ، ولا يسنّ رفع اليدين إلا عند افتتاح كل صلاة، وعند تكبير القنوت في الوتر وتكبيرات الزوائد في العيدين وحين يرى الكعبة، وحين يستلم الحجر الأسود، وحين يقوم على الصفا والمروة، وعند الوقوف بعرفة ومزدلفة، وبعد رمي الجمرة الأولى والوسطى، وعند التسبيح عقب الصلوات، وإذا فرغ الرجل من سجدتي الركعة الثانية افترش رجله اليسرى وجلس عليها، ونصب يمناه، ووجه أصابعها نحو القبلة، ووضع يديه على فخذيه وبسط أصابعه، والمرأة تتورك . للنهوض : أي للقيام للركعة الثانية مراقي الفلاح بلا اعتماد إلخ رد على الشافعي حيث ذهب إلى أنه يعتمد بيديه على الأرض ويجلس جلسة خفيفة تسمى جلسة استراحة كالأولى: أي فيما قدمناه الأركان من والواجبات والسنن والأداب (البحر الرائق لا يثني : أي لا يأتي بدعاء الاستفتاح. (البحر الرائق) ولا يسن رفع إلخ :أي أفاد أنه لا يرفع يديه على وجه السنة المؤكدة إلا في هذه المواضع، وليس مراده النفي مطلقاً؛ لأن رفع الأيدي وقت الدعاء مستحب كما عليه المسلمون في سائر البلاد. [البحر الرائق: ٦١٦/١] إلا عند افتتاح إلخ في المسألة حكاية روي أن الأوزاعي لقي أبا حنيفة في المسجد الحرام، فقال: ما بال أهل العراق لا يرفعون أيديهم عند الركوع وعند رفع الرأس منه، وقد حدثني الزهري عن سالم عن ابن عمر أنه كان يرفع يديه عندهما، فقال أبو حنيفة: حدثني حماد عن إبراهيم عن علقمة أن النبي كان يرفع يديه عند تكبيرة الافتتاح، ثم لا يعود، فقال الأوزاعي: عجبا من أبي حنيفة: أحدثه بحديث الزهري عن سالم، وهو يحدثني بحديث حماد عن إبراهيم، فرجح حديثه بعلو إسناده، فقال أبو حنيفة: أما حماد فكان أفقه من سالم، ولو لا سبق ابن عمر ما لقلت بأن علقمة أفقه منه، الزهري، وإبراهيم وأما عبد الله فعبد الله، فرجح حديثه بفقه الرواة، وهو المذهب، فإن الترجيح بفقه الرواة، لا بعلو الإسناد. [العناية: ٢٦٩/١] والكلام في هذا الموضع كثير ، وهذا المختصر لا يحتمله خلا أن المعتمد الرواة، ورواة أخبارنا البدريون من أصحاب رسول الله الذين كانوا يلون النبي في الصلاة، ورواته : ابن عمر ووائل بن حجر كانوا يقومون ببعد منه الضلع البلا، والأخذ بقول الأقرب أولى، وروي عن ابن عباس أنه قال: العشرة الذين شهد لهم النبي بالجنة لم يكونوا يرفعون أيديهم إلا عند افتتاح الصلاة. (العناية)
Then he raises his head saying takbir to stand up without leaning on the ground with his hands and without sitting. The second rak'ah is like the first, except that he does not recite Thana' nor Ta'awwudh. Raising the hands is not a emphasized Sunnah except at the opening of every prayer, at the takbir of Qunut in Witr and the extra takbirs in the two Eids, when seeing the Ka'bah, when touching the Black Stone, when standing on Safa and Marwa, at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after throwing the first and middle Jamrah, and when performing tasbih after prayers. When a man finishes the two prostrations of the second rak'ah, he sits on his left foot (iftirash), erects his right foot, points his toes towards the Qiblah, places his hands on his thighs and spreads his fingers. A woman sits in tawarruk. [Commentary:] 'to stand up': i.e. to stand for the second rak'ah (Maraqi al-Falah). 'without leaning' etc. is a refutation of al-Shafi'i who held that he leans on his hands and sits briefly called 'sitting of rest'. 'like the first': i.e. in what we have mentioned of pillars, obligatory acts, sunnahs, and etiquettes (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq). 'he does not recite Thana': i.e. he does not recite the opening supplication (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq). 'Raising the hands is not a emphasized Sunnah' etc.: i.e. it indicates that he does not raise his hands as an emphasized Sunnah except in these places. It is not meant to negate absolutely, because raising hands during supplication is recommended as Muslims practice in all lands. [Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 1:616] 'Except at the opening' etc.: There is a story in this matter: It is narrated that al-Awza'i met Abu Hanifah in the Sacred Mosque and said: 'Why do the people of Iraq not raise their hands at bowing and when raising the head from it? Indeed al-Zuhri narrated to me from Salim from Ibn 'Umar that he used to raise his hands at both.' Abu Hanifah said: 'Hammad narrated to me from Ibrahim from 'Alqamah that the Prophet used to raise his hands at the opening takbir, then did not return.' Al-Awza'i said: 'I am amazed at Abu Hanifah: I narrate to him a hadith of al-Zuhri from Salim, and he narrates to me a hadith of Hammad from Ibrahim.' He preferred his hadith by the high chain. Abu Hanifah said: 'As for Hammad, he was more knowledgeable in fiqh than Salim. Were it not for the precedence of Ibn 'Umar, I would have said that 'Alqamah is more knowledgeable than him. And as for 'Abdullah, 'Abdullah.' So he preferred his hadith by the fiqh of the narrators. That is the school, because preference is by the fiqh of the narrators, not by high chain. [Al-'Inaya 1:269] The discussion in this place is extensive, and this concise book cannot accommodate it. However, the relied-upon are the narrators, and the narrators of our reports are the Badriyyun from the companions of the Messenger of Allah who used to stand close to the Prophet in prayer. Its narrators: Ibn 'Umar and Wa'il ibn Hujr used to stand at a distance from him. Accepting the statement of the closer one is better. It is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas that he said: 'The ten who were promised Paradise by the Prophet did not raise their hands except at the opening of the prayer.' (Al-'Inaya)
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the method of standing for the second rak'ah and the occasions for raising hands in prayer. The ruling states that when rising from prostration for the second rak'ah, the worshiper says the takbir and stands up without leaning on the ground with his hands and without a brief sitting (jalsat al-istiraha). This is a refutation of Imam al-Shafi'i who held that the worshiper should lean on his hands and sit briefly. The second rak'ah is identical to the first in its pillars, wajibat, sunan, and adab, except that the opening supplication (thana') and the ta'awwudh are not repeated. The commentary clarifies that raising the hands is not a sunnah mu'akkadah except at specific points: the opening takbir of every prayer, takbir al-qunut in witr, the extra takbirs in Eid prayers, upon seeing the Ka'bah, when touching the Black Stone, when standing on Safa and Marwah, at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after throwing the first and middle jamrah, and during tasbih after prayers. However, raising hands during supplication is mustahabb. The proof includes a famous debate between al-Awza'i and Abu Hanifah in the Sacred Mosque. Al-Awza'i argued based on a hadith from al-Zuhri → Salim → Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet raised his hands at bowing and rising. Abu Hanifah countered with a hadith from Hammad → Ibrahim → 'Alqamah that the Prophet raised his hands only at the opening takbir. Abu Hanifah argued that Hammad was more knowledgeable in fiqh than Salim, and that preference in hadith is given based on the fiqh of the narrators, not merely the chain's height. He also cited that the Badri companions, who were closest to the Prophet in prayer, did not raise hands except at the opening. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (mufta bihi). The passage also briefly mentions the sitting posture after the second rak'ah: a man sits iftirash (left foot under him, right foot erect, toes pointed to Qiblah), and a woman sits tawarruk (both feet to the right side). This is part of the same unit.

Key Terms
النهوض (al-nuhud): Standing up from prostration for the next rak'ah.
لا يثني (la yuthni): He does not recite the opening supplication (Thana') in the second rak'ah.
الترجيح بفقه الرواة (al-tarjih bi-fiqh al-ruwāt): Preference given to a hadith based on the juristic knowledge of its narrators rather than the height of the chain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, at how many occasions is raising hands a sunnah mu'akkadah?
A) Only at the opening takbir
B) At the opening takbir, bowing, and rising
C) At the opening takbir, witr qunut, Eid takbirs, and other specific occasions
D) At every takbir

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts raising hands in prayer to specific occasions, relying on the hadith of 'Alqamah and the practice of the Badri companions, and rejects raising at bowing and rising.
